style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit"><tbody><tr><th scope="col" class="navbox-title" colspan="2" style="background-color:#C3D6EF;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Campaignbox_English_Civil_War" title="Template:Campaignbox English Civil War"><abbr title="View this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a style="line-height:1.6em"><a class="mw-selflink selflink"><span class="wrap">English Civil War</span></a></span></div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/First_English_Civil_War" title="First English Civil War">First</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Second_English_Civil_War" title="Second English Civil War">Second</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Third_English_Civil_War" title="Third English Civil War">Third</a></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<p>The <b>English Civil War</b> (1642\xe2\x80\x931651) was a series of armed conflicts and political machinations between Parliamentarians ("<a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Roundheads</a>") and Royalists ("<a href="/wiki/Cavalier" title="Cavalier">Cavaliers</a>") over, principally, the manner of England\'s governance. The <a href="/wiki/First_English_Civil_War" title="First English Civil War">first</a> (1642\xe2\x80\x931646) and <a href="/wiki/Second_English_Civil_War" title="Second English Civil War">second</a> (1648\xe2\x80\x931649) wars pitted the supporters of <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">King Charles I</a> against the supporters of the <a href="/wiki/Long_Parliament" title="Long Parliament">Long Parliament</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Third_English_Civil_War" title="Third English Civil War">third</a> (1649\xe2\x80\x931651) saw fighting between supporters of <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">King Charles II</a> and supporters of the <a href="/wiki/Rump_Parliament" title="Rump Parliament">Rump Parliament</a>. The war ended with the Parliamentarian victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Worcester" title="Battle of Worcester">Battle of Worcester</a> on 3 September 1651.\n</p><p>The overall outcome of the war was threefold: <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice_for_the_trial_of_Charles_I" class="mw-redirect" title="High Court of Justice for the trial of Charles I">the trial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Execution_of_Charles_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Execution of Charles I">execution of Charles I</a> (1649); the exile of his son, Charles II (1651); and the replacement of <a href="/wiki/English_monarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="English monarchy">English monarchy</a> with, at first, the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth of England</a> (1649\xe2\x80\x931653) and then <a href="/wiki/The_Protectorate" title="The Protectorate">the Protectorate</a> under the personal rule of <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> (1653\xe2\x80\x931658) and subsequently his son <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cromwell" title="Richard Cromwell">Richard</a> (1658\xe2\x80\x931659). In England, the monopoly of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> on Christian worship was ended, while in Ireland the victors consolidated the established <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Ascendancy" title="Protestant Ascendancy">Protestant Ascendancy</a>. Constitutionally, the wars established the precedent that an English monarch cannot govern without <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament</a>\'s consent, although the idea of <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_sovereignty" title="Parliamentary sovereignty">Parliament as the ruling power of England</a> was only legally established as part of the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> in 1688.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEB_staff2016a_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEB_staff2016a-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#English_overseas_possessions"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">English overseas possessions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Casualties"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Casualties</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Popular_gains"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Popular gains</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Aftermath"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Aftermath</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Historiography_and_explanations"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography and explanations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Re-enactments"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Re-enactments</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">19</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">20</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The term "English Civil War" appears most often in the singular form, although historians often divide the conflict into two or three separate wars. These wars were not restricted to <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> as <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> was a part of the Kingdom of England and was affected accordingly, and the conflicts also involved wars with, and civil wars within, both <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>. The war in all these countries is known as the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a>. In the early 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Sir Walter Scott</a> referred to it as "the Great Civil War."<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unlike other <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_civil_wars" title="List of English civil wars">civil wars in England</a>, which focused on who should rule, this war was more concerned with the manner in which the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a> were governed. The <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica_Eleventh_Edition" title="Encyclop\xc3\xa6dia Britannica Eleventh Edition">1911 <i>Encyclop\xc3\xa6dia Britannica</i></a> called the series of conflicts the "Great Rebellion",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> while some historians&#160;&#8211;&#32;especially <a href="/wiki/Marxist" class="mw-redirect" title="Marxist">Marxists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hill_(historian)" title="Christopher Hill (historian)">Christopher Hill</a> (1912\xe2\x80\x932003)&#160;&#8211;&#32;have long favoured the term "<a href="/wiki/English_Revolution" title="English Revolution">English Revolution</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The two sides had their geographical strongholds, such that minority elements were silenced or fled. The strongholds of the royalty included the countryside, the shires, and the less economically developed areas of northern and western England. On the other hand, all the <a href="/wiki/City_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="City status in the United Kingdom">cathedral cities</a> (except York, Chester, Worcester and Hereford and the royalist stronghold of Oxford) sided with Parliament. All the industrial centers, the ports, and the economically advanced regions of southern and eastern England typically were parliamentary strongholds. Lacey Baldwin Smith says, "the words <i>populous, rich, and rebellious</i> seemed to go hand in hand".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1983251_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1983251-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes1985236\xe2\x80\x9363_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes1985236\xe2\x80\x9363-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Strategy_and_tactics">Strategy and tactics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Strategy and tactics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Many of the officers and veteran soldiers of the English Civil War studied and implemented war strategies that had been learned and perfected in other wars across Europe, namely by the Spanish and the Dutch during the <a href="/wiki/Eighty_Years%27_War" title="Eighty Years&#39; War">Dutch war for independence</a> which began in 1568.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaker1986_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaker1986-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The main battle tactic came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Pike_and_shot" title="Pike and shot">pike and shot</a> infantry, in which the two sides would line up, facing each other, with infantry brigades of <a href="/wiki/Musketeer" title="Musketeer">musketeers</a> in the centre, carrying matchlock muskets; these muskets were inaccurate, but could be lethal at a range of up to 300 yards. The brigades would arrange themselves in lines of musketeers, three deep, where the first row would kneel, the second would crouch, and the third would stand, allowing all three to fire a volley simultaneously.<sup id="cite_ref-Simkin_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simkin-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> At times there would be two groups of three lines allowing one group to reload while the other group arranged themselves and fired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurneYoung1998_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurneYoung1998-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (April 2015)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Mixed in among the musketeers were pikemen carrying <a href="/wiki/Pike_(weapon)" title="Pike (weapon)">pikes</a> that were between 12 feet (4&#160;m) and 18 feet (5&#160;m) long, whose primary purpose was to protect the musketeers from cavalry charges. Positioned on each side of the infantry were the cavalry, with a right-wing led by the lieutenant-general, and a left-wing by the commissary general; the main goal of the cavalry was to rout the opponent\'s cavalry and then turn and overpower their infantry.<sup id="cite_ref-Simkin_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simkin-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (April 2017)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The Royalist cavaliers\' skill and speed on horseback led to many early victories. Prince Rupert, the leader of the king\'s cavalry, learned a tactic while fighting in the Dutch army where the cavalry would charge at full speed into the opponent\'s infantry firing their pistols just before impact.<sup id="cite_ref-Simkin_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simkin-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (April 2017)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>However, with Oliver Cromwell and the introduction of the more disciplined <a href="/wiki/New_Model_Army" title="New Model Army">New Model Army</a>, a group of disciplined pikemen would stand their ground in the face of charging cavalry and could have a devastating effect. While the Parliamentarian cavalry were slower than the cavaliers, they were also better disciplined.<sup id="cite_ref-Simkin_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simkin-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The Royalists had a tendency to chase down individual targets after the initial charge leaving their forces scattered and tired. <a href="/wiki/Ironside_(cavalry)" title="Ironside (cavalry)">Cromwell\'s cavalry</a>, on the other hand, trained to operate as a single unit, which led to many decisive victories.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="The_King.27s_rule"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_King\'s_rule">The King\'s rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The King&#039;s rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="vertical-navbox vcard" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid -0.2em 0; font-size:65%; font-weight:normal;">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:History_of_England" title="Category:History of England"><b>a series</b></a> on the</div></th>\n</tr><tr>\n<th class="vcard Staffordshire">Staffordshire</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Suffolk" title="History of Suffolk">Suffolk</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Surrey" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Surrey">Surrey</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Tyne_and_Wear" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Tyne and Wear">Tyne and Wear</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Warwickshire" title="History of Warwickshire">Warwickshire</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_West_Midlands" title="History of the West Midlands">West Midlands</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_West_Sussex" class="mw-redirect" title="History of West Sussex">West Sussex</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_West_Yorkshire" class="mw-redirect" title="History of West Yorkshire">West Yorkshire</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Wiltshire" title="History of Wiltshire">Wiltshire</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Worcestershire" title="History of Worcestershire">Worcestershire</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:#eee; text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/City_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="City status in the United Kingdom">By city or town</a></div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center"><div class="hlist">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Birmingham" title="History of Birmingham">Birmingham</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Bournemouth" title="History of Bournemouth">Bournemouth</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Brighton" title="History of Brighton">Brighton</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Bristol" title="History of Bristol">Bristol</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Chester" title="History of Chester">Chester</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Christchurch,_Dorset" title="History of Christchurch, Dorset">Christchurch</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Colchester" title="History of Colchester">Colchester</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Coventry" title="History of Coventry">Coventry</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Dover" title="History of Dover">Dover</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Folkestone" title="History of Folkestone">Folkestone</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Leeds" title="History of Leeds">Leeds</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Liverpool" title="History of Liverpool">Liverpool</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_London" title="History of London">London</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Maidstone" title="History of Maidstone">Maidstone</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Manchester" title="History of Manchester">Manchester</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Milton_Keynes" title="History of Milton Keynes">Milton Keynes</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="History of Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Nottingham" title="History of Nottingham">Nottingham</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Plymouth" title="History of Plymouth">Plymouth</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Poole" title="History of Poole">Poole</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Portsmouth" title="History of Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Reading,_Berkshire" title="History of Reading, Berkshire">Reading</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Rochester,_Kent" title="History of Rochester, Kent">Rochester</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Sheffield" title="History of Sheffield">Sheffield</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Shrewsbury" title="History of Shrewsbury">Shrewsbury</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Southampton" title="History of Southampton">Southampton</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_St_Albans" title="History of St Albans">St Albans</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Torquay" title="History of Torquay">Torquay</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Wetherby" title="History of Wetherby">Wetherby</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Worthing" title="History of Worthing">Worthing</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_York" title="History of York">York</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_England.svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of England.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/16px-Flag_of_England.svg.png" width="16" height="10" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/24px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/32px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="480" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:England" title="Portal:England">England&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_England" title="Template:History of England"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_England" title="Template talk:History of England"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_England&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The English Civil War broke out less than forty years after the death of Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a> in 1603. Elizabeth\'s death had resulted in the succession of her <a href="/wiki/Cousin_chart" class="mw-redirect" title="Cousin chart">first cousin twice-removed</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_VI_and_I" title="James VI and I">King James VI of Scotland</a>, to the English throne as James I of England, creating the first <a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">personal union of the Scottish and English kingdoms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> As King of Scots, James had become accustomed to Scotland\'s weak parliamentary tradition since assuming control of the Scottish government in 1583, so that upon assuming power south of the border, the new King of England was genuinely affronted by the constraints the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">English Parliament</a> attempted to place on him in exchange for money. In spite of this, James\' personal extravagance meant he was perennially short of money and had to resort to extra-Parliamentary sources of income.\n</p><p>This extravagance was tempered by James\' peaceful disposition, so that by the succession of his son <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a> to the English and Scottish thrones in 1625 the two kingdoms had both experienced relative peace, both internally and in their relations with each other, for as long as anyone could remember. Charles hoped to unite the kingdoms of England, Scotland and Ireland into a new single kingdom, fulfilling the dream of his father.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Many English Parliamentarians had suspicions regarding such a move because they feared that setting up a new kingdom might destroy the old English traditions which had bound the English monarchy. As Charles shared his father\'s position on the power of the crown (James had described kings as "little gods on Earth", chosen by God to rule in accordance with the doctrine of the "<a href="/wiki/Divine_Right_of_Kings" class="mw-redirect" title="Divine Right of Kings">Divine Right of Kings</a>"), the suspicions of the Parliamentarians had some justification.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Charles_I_after_original_by_van_Dyck.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/King_Charles_I_after_original_by_van_Dyck.jpg/170px-King_Charles_I_after_original_by_van_Dyck.jpg" width="170" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/King_Charles_I_after_original_by_van_Dyck.jpg/255px-King_Charles_I_after_original_by_van_Dyck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/King_Charles_I_after_original_by_van_Dyck.jpg/340px-King_Charles_I_after_original_by_van_Dyck.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2317" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Charles_I_after_original_by_van_Dyck.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Charles I, painted by Van Dyck</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliament_in_the_English_constitutional_framework">Parliament in the English constitutional framework</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Parliament in the English constitutional framework">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>At the time, the Parliament of England did not have a large permanent role in the English system of government. Instead, Parliament functioned as a temporary advisory committee and was summoned only if and when the monarch saw fit. Once summoned, a parliament\'s continued existence was at the king\'s pleasure, since it was subject to dissolution by him at any time.\n</p><p>Yet, in spite of this limited role, over the preceding centuries Parliament had acquired <i>de facto</i> powers of enough significance that monarchs could not simply ignore them indefinitely. For a monarch, Parliament\'s most indispensable power was its ability to raise tax revenues far in excess of all other sources of revenue at the Crown\'s disposal. By the seventeenth century, Parliament\'s tax-raising powers had come to be derived from the fact that the <a href="/wiki/Gentry" title="Gentry">gentry</a> was the only stratum of society with the ability and actual authority to collect and remit the most meaningful forms of taxation then available at the local level. This meant that if the king wanted to ensure a smooth collection of revenue, he needed the co-operation of the gentry. For all of the Crown\'s legal authority, by any modern standard, its resources were limited to the extent that, if and when the gentry refused to collect the king\'s taxes on a national scale, the Crown lacked any practical means with which to compel them.\n</p><p>Therefore, in order to secure their co-operation, monarchs permitted the gentry (and only the gentry) to elect representatives to sit in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_England" title="House of Commons of England">House of Commons</a>. When assembled along with the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, these elected representatives formed a Parliament. The concept of Parliaments therefore allowed representatives of the gentry to meet, primarily (at least in the opinion of the monarch) so that they could give their sanction to whatever taxes the monarch expected their electorate to collect. In the process, the representatives could also confer and send policy proposals to the king in the form of bills. However, Parliament lacked any legal means of forcing its will upon the monarch; its only leverage with the king was the threat of its withholding the financial means required to execute his plans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnston190183\xe2\x80\x9386_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnston190183\xe2\x80\x9386-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliamentary_concerns_and_the_Petition_of_Right">Parliamentary concerns and the Petition of Right</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Parliamentary concerns and the Petition of Right">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Peter_Lely_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Sir_Peter_Lely_001.jpg/170px-Sir_Peter_Lely_001.jpg" width="170" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Sir_Peter_Lely_001.jpg/255px-Sir_Peter_Lely_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Sir_Peter_Lely_001.jpg/340px-Sir_Peter_Lely_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1576" data-file-height="2125" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Peter_Lely_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henrietta_Maria_of_France" title="Henrietta Maria of France">Henrietta Maria</a>, painted by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lely" title="Peter Lely">Peter Lely</a>, 1660</div></div></div>\n<p>Many concerns were raised over Charles\'s marriage to a <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic</a>, French princess <a href="/wiki/Henrietta_Maria_of_France" title="Henrietta Maria of France">Henrietta Maria</a>, in 1625. The Parliament refused to assign him the traditional right to collect customs duties for his entire reign, deciding instead to grant it only on a provisional basis and negotiate with him.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Charles, meanwhile, decided to send an expeditionary force to relieve the French <a href="/wiki/Huguenots" title="Huguenots">Huguenots</a> whom French royal troops held <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_La_Rochelle" title="Siege of La Rochelle">besieged in La Rochelle</a>. Military support for Protestants on the Continent had the potential to alleviate concerns brought about by the King\'s marriage to a Catholic. However, Charles\'s insistence on having his unpopular royal favourite <a href="/wiki/George_Villiers,_1st_Duke_of_Buckingham" title="George Villiers, 1st Duke of Buckingham">George Villiers, the Duke of Buckingham</a>, assume command of the English force undermined that support. Unfortunately for Charles and Buckingham, the relief expedition proved a fiasco (1627),<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> and Parliament, already hostile to Buckingham for his monopoly on <a href="/wiki/Patronage" title="Patronage">royal patronage</a>, opened <a href="/wiki/Impeachment" title="Impeachment">impeachment</a> proceedings against him.<sup id="cite_ref-Gregg-175_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregg-175-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Charles responded by dissolving Parliament. This move, while saving Buckingham, reinforced the impression that Charles wanted to avoid Parliamentary scrutiny of his ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-Gregg-175_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregg-175-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Having dissolved Parliament and unable to raise money without it, the king assembled a new one in 1628. (The elected members included <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Coke" title="Edward Coke">Edward Coke</a>.) The new Parliament drew up the <a href="/wiki/Petition_of_Right" title="Petition of Right">Petition of Right</a>, and Charles accepted it as a concession in order to obtain his subsidy.<sup id="cite_ref-Purkiss_2007_93_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Purkiss_2007_93-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Among other things, the Petition referred to <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> However, it did not grant him the right of <a href="/wiki/Tonnage_and_poundage" title="Tonnage and poundage">tonnage and poundage</a>, which Charles had been collecting without Parliamentary authorisation since 1625.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESommerville199265,_71,_80_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESommerville199265,_71,_80-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Several of the more active members of the opposition were imprisoned, which caused some outrage;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESommerville199265,_71,_80_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESommerville199265,_71,_80-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> one, <a href="/wiki/John_Eliot_(statesman)" title="John Eliot (statesman)">John Eliot</a>, subsequently died in prison, becoming regarded as a martyr for the rights of Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1998417_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1998417-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_rule">Personal rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Personal rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Charles I avoided calling a Parliament for the next decade, a period known as the "<a href="/wiki/Personal_rule_of_Charles_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Personal rule of Charles I">personal rule of Charles I</a>", or the "Eleven Years\' Tyranny".<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> During this period, Charles\'s lack of money determined policies. First and foremost, to avoid Parliament, the King needed to avoid war. Charles made peace with France and Spain, effectively ending England\'s involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years&#39; War">Thirty Years\' War</a>. However, that in itself was far from enough to balance the Crown\'s finances.\n</p><p>Unable to raise revenue without Parliament and unwilling to convene it, Charles resorted to other means. One method was reviving certain conventions, often long-outdated. For example, a failure to attend and to receive <a href="/wiki/Knighthood" class="mw-redirect" title="Knighthood">knighthood</a> at Charles\'s coronation was a finable offence with the fine paid to the Crown. The King also tried to raise revenue through the <a href="/wiki/Ship_money" title="Ship money">ship money</a> tax, by exploiting a naval-war scare in 1635, demanding that the inland English counties pay the tax for the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>. Established law supported this policy, but authorities had ignored it for centuries, and many regarded it as yet another extra-Parliamentary (and therefore illegal) tax.<sup id="cite_ref-Pipes-143_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pipes-143-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Some prominent men refused to pay ship money, arguing that the tax was illegal, but they lost in court, and the fines imposed on them for refusing to pay ship money (and for standing against the tax\'s legality) aroused widespread indignation.<sup id="cite_ref-Pipes-143_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pipes-143-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the "Personal Rule", Charles aroused most antagonism through his religious measures: he believed in <a href="/wiki/High_church" title="High church">High Anglicanism</a>, a sacramental version of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, theologically based upon <a href="/wiki/Arminianism_in_the_Church_of_England" title="Arminianism in the Church of England">Arminianism</a>, a creed shared with his main political advisor, Archbishop <a href="/wiki/William_Laud" title="William Laud">William Laud</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In 1633, Charles appointed Laud as <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a> and started making the Church more ceremonial, replacing the wooden <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)#Communion_rite" title="Mass (liturgy)">communion</a> tables with stone altars.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Puritans accused Laud of reintroducing Catholicism; when they complained, he had them arrested. In 1637, <a href="/wiki/John_Bastwick" title="John Bastwick">John Bastwick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Burton_(Puritan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Burton (Puritan)">Henry Burton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Prynne" title="William Prynne">William Prynne</a> had their ears cut off for writing pamphlets attacking Laud\'s views\xe2\x80\x94a rare penalty for <a href="/wiki/Gentleman" title="Gentleman">gentlemen</a>, and one that aroused anger.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, the Church authorities revived the statutes passed in the time of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a> about church attendance and fined Puritans for not attending Anglican church services.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rebellion_in_Scotland">Rebellion in Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Rebellion in Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bishops%27_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishops&#39; War">Bishops\' War</a></div>\n<p>The end of Charles\'s independent governance came when he attempted to apply the same religious policies in Scotland. The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>, reluctantly <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_polity" title="Episcopal polity">episcopal</a> in structure, had independent traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Charles, however, wanted one uniform Church throughout Britain<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and introduced a new, High Anglican version of the English <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a> to Scotland in the middle of 1637. This was violently resisted; a riot broke out in Edinburgh,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> which may have been started in <a href="/wiki/St_Giles%27_Cathedral" title="St Giles&#39; Cathedral">St Giles\' Cathedral</a>, according to legend, by <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Geddes" title="Jenny Geddes">Jenny Geddes</a>. In February 1638, the Scots formulated their objections to royal policy in the <a href="/wiki/National_Covenant" class="mw-redirect" title="National Covenant">National Covenant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> This document took the form of a "loyal protest", rejecting all innovations not first having been tested by free parliaments and General Assemblies of the Church.\n</p><p>In the spring of 1639, King Charles I accompanied his forces to the Scottish border to end the rebellion known as the <a href="/wiki/Bishops%27_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishops&#39; War">Bishops\' War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Purkiss-96_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Purkiss-96-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> But, after an inconclusive military campaign, he accepted the offered Scottish truce: the <a href="/wiki/Pacification_of_Berwick" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacification of Berwick">Pacification of Berwick</a>. The truce proved temporary, and a second war followed in the middle of 1640. This time, a Scots army defeated Charles\'s forces in the north, then captured <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Charles eventually agreed not to interfere with Scotland\'s religion and paid the Scots\' war-expenses.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recall_of_the_English_Parliament">Recall of the English Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Recall of the English Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Charles needed to suppress the rebellion in Scotland. He had insufficient funds, however, and needed to seek money from a newly elected <a href="/wiki/Short_Parliament" title="Short Parliament">English Parliament</a> in 1640.<sup id="cite_ref-Coward-180_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-180-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The majority faction in the new Parliament, led by <a href="/wiki/John_Pym" title="John Pym">John Pym</a>, took this appeal for money as an opportunity to discuss grievances against the Crown and opposed the idea of an English invasion of Scotland. Charles took exception to this <i><a href="/wiki/Lese_majesty" class="mw-redirect" title="Lese majesty">l\xc3\xa8se-majest\xc3\xa9</a></i> (offence against the ruler) and dissolved the Parliament after only a few weeks; hence the name "the <a href="/wiki/Short_Parliament" title="Short Parliament">Short Parliament</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Coward-180_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-180-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Without Parliament\'s support, Charles attacked Scotland again, breaking the truce at Berwick, and suffered a comprehensive defeat. The Scots went on to invade England, occupying <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Durham,_England" title="Durham, England">Durham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Coward-180_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-180-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, another of Charles\' chief advisors, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wentworth,_1st_Earl_of_Strafford" title="Thomas Wentworth, 1st Earl of Strafford">Thomas Wentworth, 1st Viscount Wentworth</a>, had risen to the role of Lord Deputy of Ireland in 1632<sup id="cite_ref-Purkiss-89_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Purkiss-89-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> and brought in much-needed revenue for Charles by persuading the Irish Catholic gentry to pay new taxes in return for promised religious concessions.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1639, Charles had recalled Wentworth to England and in 1640 made him Earl of Strafford, attempting to have him achieve similar results in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Purkiss-89_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Purkiss-89-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> This time he proved less successful and the English forces fled the field in their second encounter with the Scots in 1640.<sup id="cite_ref-Purkiss-89_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Purkiss-89-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Almost the entirety of Northern England was occupied and Charles was forced to pay \xc2\xa3850 per day to keep the Scots from advancing. If he did not, they would "take" the money by pillaging and burning the cities and towns of Northern England.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>All this put Charles in a desperate financial position. As King of Scots, he had to find money to pay the Scottish army in England; as King of England, he had to find money to pay and equip an English army to defend England. His means of raising English revenue without an English Parliament fell critically short of achieving this.<sup id="cite_ref-Purkiss_2007_93_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Purkiss_2007_93-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Against this backdrop, and according to advice from the <a href="/wiki/Magnum_Concilium" title="Magnum Concilium">Magnum Concilium</a> (the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, but without the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_England" title="House of Commons of England">Commons</a>, so not a Parliament), Charles finally bowed to pressure and summoned another English Parliament in November 1640.<sup id="cite_ref-Purkiss-96_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Purkiss-96-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Long_Parliament">The Long Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: The Long Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Long_Parliament" title="Long Parliament">Long Parliament</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LongParliament.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/LongParliament.jpg/200px-LongParliament.jpg" width="200" height="318" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/LongParliament.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="285" data-file-height="453" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LongParliament.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Session of the Long Parliament</div></div></div>\n<p>The new Parliament proved even more hostile to Charles than its predecessor. It immediately began to discuss grievances against Charles and his government and with Pym and <a href="/wiki/John_Hampden" title="John Hampden">Hampden</a> (of <a href="/wiki/Ship_money" title="Ship money">ship money</a> fame) in the lead, took the opportunity presented by the King\'s troubles to force various reforming measures\xe2\x80\x94including many with strong "anti-Papist" themes\xe2\x80\x94upon him.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The legislators passed a law which stated that a new Parliament should convene at least once every three years\xe2\x80\x94without the King\'s summons, if necessary. Other laws passed by the Parliament made it illegal for the king to impose taxes without Parliamentary consent and later gave Parliament control over the king\'s ministers. Finally, the Parliament passed a law forbidding the King to dissolve it without its consent, even if the three years were up. Ever since, this Parliament has been known as the "Long Parliament". However, Parliament did attempt to avert conflict by requiring all adults to sign <a href="/wiki/Protestation_of_1641" title="Protestation of 1641">The Protestation</a>, an oath of allegiance to Charles.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Early in the Long Parliament\'s proceedings the house overwhelmingly accused <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wentworth,_1st_Earl_of_Strafford" title="Thomas Wentworth, 1st Earl of Strafford">Thomas Wentworth, Earl of Strafford</a> of high treason and other crimes and misdemeanours.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Henry_Vane_the_Younger" title="Henry Vane the Younger">Henry Vane the Younger</a> supplied evidence in relation to Strafford\'s claimed improper use of the army in Ireland, alleging that Strafford was encouraging the King to use his army raised in Ireland to threaten England into compliance. This evidence was obtained from Vane\'s father, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Vane_the_Elder" title="Henry Vane the Elder">Henry Vane the Elder</a>, a member of the King\'s Privy council, who refused to confirm it in Parliament out of loyalty to Charles. On 10 April 1641, Pym\'s case collapsed, but Pym made a direct appeal to <a href="/wiki/Henry_Vane_the_Younger" title="Henry Vane the Younger">Henry Vane the Younger</a> to produce a copy of the notes from the King\'s Privy council, discovered by the younger Vane and secretly turned over to Pym, to the great anguish of the Elder Vane.<sup id="cite_ref-Upham187_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Upham187-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> These notes from the King\'s Privy Council contained evidence Strafford had told the King, "Sir, you have done your duty, and your subjects have failed in theirs; and therefore you are absolved from the rules of government, and may supply yourself by extraordinary ways; you have an army in Ireland, with which you may reduce the kingdom."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpham1842187_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpham1842187-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHibbert1968154_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHibbert1968154-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995224_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995224-47">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pym immediately launched a <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_attainder" title="Bill of attainder">Bill of Attainder</a>, stating Strafford\'s guilt and demanding that the Earl be put to death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995224_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995224-47">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Unlike a guilty finding in a court case, attainder did not require a <a href="/wiki/Legal_burden_of_proof" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal burden of proof">legal burden of proof</a>, but it did require <a href="/wiki/Royal_Assent" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Assent">the king\'s approval</a>. Charles, however, guaranteed Strafford that he would not sign the attainder, without which the bill could not be passed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995225_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995225-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the Lords were opposed to the severity of the sentence of death imposed upon Strafford. Yet, increased tensions and <a href="/wiki/First_Army_Plot" class="mw-redirect" title="First Army Plot">a plot</a> in the army to support Strafford began to sway the issue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995225_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995225-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> On 21 April, the Commons passed the Bill (204 in favour, 59 opposed, and 250 abstained),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1999123_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1999123-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and the Lords acquiesced. Charles, still incensed over the Commons\' handling of Buckingham, refused. Strafford himself, hoping to head off the war he saw looming, wrote to the king and asked him to reconsider.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbbott&#39;&#39;Downfall&#39;&#39;_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbbott&#39;&#39;Downfall&#39;&#39;-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Charles, fearing for the safety of his family, signed on 10 May.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1999123_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1999123-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Strafford was beheaded two days later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoward1994191_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoward1994191-51">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In the meantime both Parliament and the King agreed to an independent investigation into the king\'s involvement in Strafford\'s plot.\n</p><p>The Long Parliament then passed the <a href="/wiki/Triennial_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Triennial Act">Triennial Act</a>, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Dissolution Act">Dissolution Act</a> in May 1641, to which the Royal Assent was readily granted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlton1995222_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlton1995222-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978127_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978127-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The Triennial Act required that Parliament be summoned at least once every three years, and that when the King failed to issue proper summons, the members could assemble on their own. This act also forbade ship money without Parliament\'s consent, fines in destraint of knighthood and forced loans. Monopolies were cut back severely, and the Courts of <a href="/wiki/Star_Chamber" title="Star Chamber">Star Chamber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Court_of_High_Commission" title="Court of High Commission">High Commission</a> were abolished by the <a href="/wiki/Habeas_Corpus_Act_1640" title="Habeas Corpus Act 1640">Habeas Corpus Act 1640</a> and the Triennial Act respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGregg1981335_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGregg1981335-54">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> All remaining forms of taxation were legalised and regulated by the Tonnage and Poundage Act.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978129_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978129-55">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> On 3 May, Parliament decreed <a href="/wiki/Protestation_of_1641" title="Protestation of 1641">The Protestation</a>, attacking the \'wicked counsels\' of Charles\'s government, whereby those who signed the petition undertook to defend \'the true reformed religion\', parliament, and the king\'s person, honour and estate. Throughout May, the House of Commons launched several bills attacking bishops and episcopalianism in general, each time defeated in the Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978130_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenyon1978130-56">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbbott&#39;&#39;Downfall&#39;&#39;_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbbott&#39;&#39;Downfall&#39;&#39;-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was hoped by both Charles and Parliament that the execution of Strafford and the Protestation would end the drift towards war; in fact, they encouraged it. Charles and his supporters continued to resent Parliament\'s demands, while Parliamentarians continued to suspect Charles of wanting to impose episcopalianism and unfettered royal rule by military force. Within months, the Irish Catholics, fearing a resurgence of Protestant power, <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1641" title="Irish Rebellion of 1641">struck first</a>, and all Ireland soon descended into chaos.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Rumours circulated that the King supported the Irish, and Puritan members of the Commons soon started murmuring that this exemplified the fate that Charles had in store for them all.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In early January 1642, accompanied by 400 soldiers, Charles attempted to arrest five members of the House of Commons on a charge of treason.<sup id="cite_ref-Sherwood-1997-41_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherwood-1997-41-59">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> This attempt failed. When the troops marched into Parliament, Charles enquired of <a href="/wiki/William_Lenthall" title="William Lenthall">William Lenthall</a>, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Speakers_of_the_House_of_Commons_of_England" title="List of Speakers of the House of Commons of England">Speaker</a>, as to the whereabouts of the five. Lenthall replied, "May it please your Majesty, I have neither eyes to see nor tongue to speak in this place but as the House is pleased to direct me, whose servant I am here."<sup id="cite_ref-Sherwood-1997-41_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherwood-1997-41-59">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In other words, the Speaker proclaimed himself a servant of Parliament, rather than of the King.<sup id="cite_ref-Sherwood-1997-41_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherwood-1997-41-59">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_grievances">Local grievances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Local grievances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the summer of 1642 these national troubles helped to polarise opinion, ending indecision about which side to support or what action to take. Opposition to Charles also arose owing to many local grievances. For example, the imposition of drainage schemes in <a href="/wiki/The_Fens" title="The Fens">The Fens</a> negatively affected the livelihood of thousands of people after the King awarded a number of drainage contracts.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Many regarded the King as indifferent to public welfare, and this played a role in bringing a large part of eastern England into the Parliamentarian camp. This sentiment brought with it people such as the <a href="/wiki/Edward_Montagu,_2nd_Earl_of_Manchester" title="Edward Montagu, 2nd Earl of Manchester">Earl of Manchester</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>, each a notable wartime adversary of the King. Conversely, one of the leading drainage contractors, the <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bertie,_1st_Earl_of_Lindsey" title="Robert Bertie, 1st Earl of Lindsey">Earl of Lindsey</a>, was to die fighting for the King at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Edgehill" title="Battle of Edgehill">Battle of Edgehill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="First_English_Civil_War_.281642.E2.80.931646.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_English_Civil_War_(1642\xe2\x80\x931646)">First English Civil War (1642\xe2\x80\x931646)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: First English Civil War (1642\xe2\x80\x931646)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_English_Civil_War" title="First English Civil War">First English Civil War</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:English_civil_war_map_1642_to_1645.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/English_civil_war_map_1642_to_1645.JPG/220px-English_civil_war_map_1642_to_1645.JPG" width="220" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/English_civil_war_map_1642_to_1645.JPG/330px-English_civil_war_map_1642_to_1645.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/English_civil_war_map_1642_to_1645.JPG/440px-English_civil_war_map_1642_to_1645.JPG 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="627" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:English_civil_war_map_1642_to_1645.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Maps of territory held by Royalists (red) and Parliamentarians (yellow-green), 1642\xe2\x80\x931645</div></div></div>\n<p>In early January 1642, a few days after his failure to capture five members of the House of Commons, fearing for the safety of his family and retinue, Charles left the London area for the north of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Further negotiations by frequent correspondence between the King and the <a href="/wiki/Long_Parliament" title="Long Parliament">Long Parliament</a> through to early summer proved fruitless. As the summer progressed, cities and towns declared their sympathies for one faction or the other: for example, the garrison of Portsmouth under the command of Sir <a href="/wiki/George_Goring,_Lord_Goring" title="George Goring, Lord Goring">George Goring</a> declared for the King,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> but when Charles tried to acquire arms for his cause from <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Hull" title="Kingston upon Hull">Kingston upon Hull</a>, the depository for the weapons used in the previous Scottish campaigns, Sir <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_Hotham,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir John Hotham, 1st Baronet">John Hotham</a>, the military governor appointed by Parliament in January, refused to let Charles enter Hull,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and when Charles returned with more men later, <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Hull_(1642)" title="Siege of Hull (1642)">Hotham drove them off</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wedgwood_1970_100_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wedgwood_1970_100-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Charles issued a warrant for Hotham to be arrested as a traitor but was powerless to enforce it. Throughout the summer months, tensions rose and there was brawling in a number of places, with the first death from the conflict taking place in Manchester.<sup id="cite_ref-Wedgwood_1970_100_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wedgwood_1970_100-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the outset of the conflict, much of the country remained neutral, though the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> and most English cities favoured Parliament, while the King found considerable support in rural communities. Historians estimate that between them, both sides had only about 15,000 men.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> However, the war quickly spread and eventually involved every level of society. Many areas attempted to remain neutral. Some formed bands of <a href="/wiki/Clubmen" title="Clubmen">Clubmen</a> to protect their localities against the worst excesses of the armies of both sides,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> but most found it impossible to withstand both the King and Parliament. On one side, the King and his supporters fought for traditional government in Church and state. On the other, most supporters of the Parliamentary cause initially took up arms to defend what they thought of as the traditional balance of government in Church and state, which the bad advice the King had received from his advisers had undermined before and during the "Eleven Years\' Tyranny". The views of the members of parliament ranged from unquestioning support of the King\xe2\x80\x94at one point during the First Civil War, more members of the Commons and Lords gathered in the King\'s <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Parliament_(1644)" title="Oxford Parliament (1644)">Oxford Parliament</a> than at <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Westminster</a>\xe2\x80\x94through to radicals, who wanted major reforms in favour of <a href="/wiki/Nonconformism" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonconformism">religious independence</a> and the redistribution of power at the national level. However, even the most radical supporters of the Parliamentarian cause still favoured the retention of Charles on the throne.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="For all information since the last citation (May 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Hull_(1642)" title="Siege of Hull (1642)">debacle at Hull</a>, Charles moved on to <a href="/wiki/Nottingham" title="Nottingham">Nottingham</a>, where on 22 August 1642, he raised the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Standard_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Standard of England">royal standard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> When he raised his standard, Charles had with him about 2,000 cavalry and a small number of Yorkshire infantry-men, and using the archaic system of a <a href="/wiki/Commission_of_Array" class="mw-redirect" title="Commission of Array">Commission of Array</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Charles\'s supporters started to build a larger army around the standard. Charles moved in a south-westerly direction, first to <a href="/wiki/Stafford" title="Stafford">Stafford</a>, and then on to <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury" title="Shrewsbury">Shrewsbury</a>, because the support for his cause seemed particularly strong in the <a href="/wiki/River_Severn" title="River Severn">Severn</a> valley area and in <a href="/wiki/North_Wales" title="North Wales">North Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> While passing through <a href="/wiki/Wellington,_Shropshire" title="Wellington, Shropshire">Wellington</a>, in what became known as the "<a href="/wiki/Wellington_Declaration" title="Wellington Declaration">Wellington Declaration</a>", he declared that he would uphold the "Protestant religion, the laws of England, and the liberty of Parliament".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg/220px-Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg/330px-Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg/440px-Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2916" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Parliamentarians who opposed the King had not remained passive during this pre-war period. As in the case of Kingston upon Hull, they had taken measures to secure strategic towns and cities by appointing to office men sympathetic to their cause, and on 9 June they had voted to raise an army of 10,000 volunteers and appointed <a href="/wiki/Robert_Devereux,_3rd_Earl_of_Essex" title="Robert Devereux, 3rd Earl of Essex">Robert Devereux, 3rd Earl of Essex</a> commander three days later.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> He received orders "to rescue His Majesty\'s person, and the persons of the <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Prince [of Wales]</a> and the <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">Duke of York</a> out of the hands of those desperate persons who were about them".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Lord_Lieutenant" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Lieutenant">Lords Lieutenant</a>, whom Parliament appointed, used the <a href="/wiki/Militia_Ordinance" title="Militia Ordinance">Militia Ordinance</a> to order the militia to join Essex\'s army.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Two weeks after the King had raised his standard at Nottingham, Essex led his army north towards <a href="/wiki/Northampton" title="Northampton">Northampton</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> picking up support along the way (including a detachment of <a href="/wiki/Cambridgeshire" title="Cambridgeshire">Cambridgeshire</a> cavalry raised and commanded by <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> By the middle of September Essex\'s forces had grown to 21,000 infantry and 4,200 cavalry and <a href="/wiki/Dragoon" title="Dragoon">dragoons</a>. On 14 September he moved his army to <a href="/wiki/Coventry" title="Coventry">Coventry</a> and then to the north of the <a href="/wiki/Cotswolds" title="Cotswolds">Cotswolds</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> a strategy which placed his army between the Royalists and London. With the size of both armies now in the tens of thousands, and only Worcestershire between them, it was inevitable that cavalry reconnaissance units would sooner or later meet. This happened in the first major skirmish of the Civil War, when a cavalry troop of about 1,000 Royalists commanded by <a href="/wiki/Prince_Rupert" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Rupert">Prince Rupert</a>, a German nephew of the King and one of the outstanding cavalry commanders of the war,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> defeated a Parliamentary cavalry detachment under the command of Colonel John Brown in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Powick_Bridge" title="Battle of Powick Bridge">Battle of Powick Bridge</a>, at a bridge across the <a href="/wiki/River_Teme" title="River Teme">River Teme</a> close to <a href="/wiki/Worcester" title="Worcester">Worcester</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rupert_of_the_Rhine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Rupert_of_the_Rhine.jpg/220px-Rupert_of_the_Rhine.jpg" width="220" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Rupert_of_the_Rhine.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="283" data-file-height="367" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rupert_of_the_Rhine.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Prince_Rupert_of_the_Rhine" title="Prince Rupert of the Rhine">Prince Rupert of the Rhine</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Rupert withdrew to Shrewsbury, where a council-of-war discussed two courses of action: whether to advance towards Essex\'s new position near <a href="/wiki/Worcester" title="Worcester">Worcester</a>, or to march along the now opened road towards London. The Council decided to take the London route, but not to avoid a battle, for the Royalist generals wanted to fight Essex before he grew too strong, and the temper of both sides made it impossible to postpone the decision. In the <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hyde,_1st_Earl_of_Clarendon" title="Edward Hyde, 1st Earl of Clarendon">Earl of Clarendon\'s</a> words: "it was considered more counsellable to march towards London, it being morally sure that the earl of Essex would put himself in their way".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911404_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911404-80">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Accordingly, the army left Shrewsbury on 12 October, gaining two days\' start on the enemy, and moved south-east. This had the desired effect, as it forced Essex to move to intercept them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911404_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911404-80">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first <a href="/wiki/Pitched_battle" title="Pitched battle">pitched battle</a> of the war, fought at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Edgehill" title="Battle of Edgehill">Edgehill</a> on 23 October 1642, proved inconclusive, and both the Royalists and Parliamentarians claimed it as a victory.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The second field action of the war, the stand-off at <a href="/wiki/Turnham_Green_(Battle)" class="mw-redirect" title="Turnham Green (Battle)">Turnham Green</a>, saw Charles forced to withdraw to <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> This city would serve as his base for the remainder of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1643, the Royalist forces won at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Adwalton_Moor" title="Battle of Adwalton Moor">Adwalton Moor</a>, and gained control of most of <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In the Midlands, a Parliamentary force under <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_Gell,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir John Gell, 1st Baronet">Sir John Gell</a> besieged and captured the cathedral city of <a href="/wiki/Lichfield" title="Lichfield">Lichfield</a>, after the death of the original commander, Lord Brooke.<sup id="cite_ref-Wanklyn-74_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wanklyn-74-85">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> This group subsequently joined forces with Sir John Brereton to fight the inconclusive <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hopton_Heath" title="Battle of Hopton Heath">Battle of Hopton Heath</a> (19 March 1643), where the Royalist commander, the <a href="/wiki/Spencer_Compton,_2nd_Earl_of_Northampton" title="Spencer Compton, 2nd Earl of Northampton">Earl of Northampton</a>, was killed.<sup id="cite_ref-Wanklyn-74_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wanklyn-74-85">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Subsequent battles in the west of England at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lansdowne" title="Battle of Lansdowne">Lansdowne</a> and at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Roundway_Down" title="Battle of Roundway Down">Roundway Down</a> also went to the Royalists.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Prince Rupert could then take <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a>. In the same year, Oliver Cromwell formed his troop of "<a href="/wiki/Ironside_(cavalry)" title="Ironside (cavalry)">Ironsides</a>", a disciplined unit that demonstrated his military leadership ability. With their assistance, he won a victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gainsborough" title="Battle of Gainsborough">Battle of Gainsborough</a> in July.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At this stage, from 7 to 9 August 1643, there were some popular demonstrations in London\xe2\x80\x94both pro and against war. They were protesting at Westminster. A peace demonstration by London women, which turned violent, was suppressed by <a href="/wiki/William_Waller" title="William Waller">William Waller</a>\'s regiment of horse. Some women were beaten and even killed, and many arrested.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following these events of August, the representative of Venice in England reported to the doge that the London government took considerable measures to stifle dissent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorton2011p._~93_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorton2011p._~93-89">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In general, the early part of the war went well for the Royalists. The turning point came in the late summer and early autumn of 1643, when the Earl of Essex\'s army forced the king to raise the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Gloucester" title="Siege of Gloucester">siege of Gloucester</a><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> and then brushed the Royalist army aside at the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Newbury" title="First Battle of Newbury">First Battle of Newbury</a> (20 September 1643),<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> in order to return triumphantly to London. Other Parliamentarian forces won the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Winceby" title="Battle of Winceby">Battle of Winceby</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> giving them control of <a href="/wiki/Lincoln,_England" title="Lincoln, England">Lincoln</a>. Political manoeuvring to gain an advantage in numbers led Charles to negotiate a ceasefire in Ireland, freeing up English troops to fight on the Royalist side in England,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> while Parliament offered concessions to the Scots in return for aid and assistance.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Marston_Moor,_1644.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Battle_of_Marston_Moor%2C_1644.png/220px-Battle_of_Marston_Moor%2C_1644.png" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Battle_of_Marston_Moor%2C_1644.png/330px-Battle_of_Marston_Moor%2C_1644.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Battle_of_Marston_Moor%2C_1644.png/440px-Battle_of_Marston_Moor%2C_1644.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="399" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Marston_Moor,_1644.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marston_Moor" title="Battle of Marston Moor">Battle of Marston Moor</a>, 1644</div></div></div>\n<p>With the help of the Scots, Parliament won at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marston_Moor" title="Battle of Marston Moor">Marston Moor</a> (2 July 1644),<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> gaining <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a> and the north of England.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Cromwell\'s conduct in this battle proved decisive,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> and demonstrated his potential as both a political and an important military leader. The defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lostwithiel" title="Battle of Lostwithiel">Battle of Lostwithiel</a> in Cornwall, however, marked a serious reverse for Parliament in the south-west of England.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Subsequent fighting around <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Newbury" title="Second Battle of Newbury">Newbury</a> (27 October 1644), though tactically indecisive, strategically gave another check to Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1645, Parliament reaffirmed its determination to fight the war to a finish.
