It passed the <a href="/wiki/Self-denying_Ordinance" title="Self-denying Ordinance">Self-denying Ordinance</a>, by which all members of either House of Parliament laid down their commands, and re-organized its main forces into the <a href="/wiki/New_Model_Army" title="New Model Army">New Model Army</a>, under the command of Sir <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Fairfax,_3rd_Baron_Fairfax_of_Cameron" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Fairfax, 3rd Baron Fairfax of Cameron">Thomas Fairfax</a>, with Cromwell as his second-in-command and <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_General" class="mw-redirect" title="Lieutenant General">Lieutenant-General</a> of Horse.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> In two decisive engagements\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Naseby" title="Battle of Naseby">Battle of Naseby</a> on 14 June and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Langport" title="Battle of Langport">Battle of Langport</a> on 10 July\xe2\x80\x94the Parliamentarians effectively destroyed Charles\' armies.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the remains of his English realm Charles attempted to recover a stable base of support by consolidating the <a href="/wiki/English_Midlands" class="mw-redirect" title="English Midlands">Midlands</a>. He began to form an axis between Oxford and <a href="/wiki/Newark_on_Trent" class="mw-redirect" title="Newark on Trent">Newark on Trent</a> in Nottinghamshire. Those towns had become fortresses and showed more reliable loyalty to him than to others. He took <a href="/wiki/Leicester" title="Leicester">Leicester</a>, which lies between them, but found his resources exhausted. Having little opportunity to replenish them, in May 1646 <a href="/wiki/Charles_I%27s_journey_from_Oxford_to_the_Scottish_army_camp_near_Newark" title="Charles I&#39;s journey from Oxford to the Scottish army camp near Newark">he sought shelter</a> with a Presbyterian Scottish army at <a href="/wiki/Southwell,_Nottinghamshire" title="Southwell, Nottinghamshire">Southwell</a> in Nottinghamshire.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Charles was eventually handed over to the English Parliament by the Scots and was imprisoned.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> This marked the end of the First English Civil War.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span id="Second_English_Civil_War_.281648.E2.80.931649.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_English_Civil_War_(1648\xe2\x80\x931649)">Second English Civil War (1648\xe2\x80\x931649)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Second English Civil War (1648\xe2\x80\x931649)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_English_Civil_War" title="Second English Civil War">Second English Civil War</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Frederick_Yeames_-_And_when_did_you_last_see_your_father%3F_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/William_Frederick_Yeames_-_And_when_did_you_last_see_your_father%3F_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-William_Frederick_Yeames_-_And_when_did_you_last_see_your_father%3F_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/William_Frederick_Yeames_-_And_when_did_you_last_see_your_father%3F_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-William_Frederick_Yeames_-_And_when_did_you_last_see_your_father%3F_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/William_Frederick_Yeames_-_And_when_did_you_last_see_your_father%3F_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-William_Frederick_Yeames_-_And_when_did_you_last_see_your_father%3F_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3913" data-file-height="2015" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Frederick_Yeames_-_And_when_did_you_last_see_your_father%3F_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"And when did you last see your father?" by <a href="/wiki/William_Frederick_Yeames" title="William Frederick Yeames">William Frederick Yeames</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Charles I took advantage of the deflection of attention away from himself to negotiate a secret treaty with the Scots, again promising church reform, on 28 December 1647.<sup id="cite_ref-Seel-64_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seel-64-103">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Under the agreement, called the "<a href="/wiki/Engagers" title="Engagers">Engagement</a>", the Scots undertook to invade England on Charles\' behalf and restore him to the throne on condition of the establishment of Presbyterianism for three years.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A series of Royalist uprisings throughout England and a Scottish invasion occurred in the summer of 1648. Forces loyal to Parliament<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> put down most of the uprisings in England after little more than skirmishes, but uprisings in Kent, Essex and Cumberland, the rebellion in Wales, and the Scottish invasion involved the fighting of pitched battles and prolonged sieges.<sup id="cite_ref-Seel-64_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seel-64-103">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the spring of 1648 unpaid Parliamentarian troops in Wales changed sides. Colonel <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Horton_(soldier)" title="Thomas Horton (soldier)">Thomas Horton</a> defeated the Royalist rebels at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_St_Fagans" title="Battle of St Fagans">Battle of St Fagans</a> (8 May)<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> and the rebel leaders surrendered to Cromwell on 11 July after the protracted two-month <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Pembroke" title="Siege of Pembroke">siege of Pembroke</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sir_Thomas_Fairfax" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Thomas Fairfax">Sir Thomas Fairfax</a> defeated a Royalist uprising in Kent at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Maidstone" title="Battle of Maidstone">Battle of Maidstone</a> on 1 June. Fairfax, after his success at <a href="/wiki/Maidstone" title="Maidstone">Maidstone</a> and the pacification of <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a>, turned northward to reduce <a href="/wiki/Essex" title="Essex">Essex</a>, where, under their ardent, experienced and popular leader Sir <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lucas" title="Charles Lucas">Charles Lucas</a>, the Royalists had taken up arms in great numbers. Fairfax soon drove the enemy into <a href="/wiki/Colchester" title="Colchester">Colchester</a>, but his first attack on the town met with a repulse and he had to settle down to <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Colchester" title="Siege of Colchester">a long siege</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the North of England, Major-General <a href="/wiki/John_Lambert_(General)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Lambert (General)">John Lambert</a> fought a very successful campaign against a number of Royalist uprisings\xe2\x80\x94the largest that of Sir <a href="/wiki/Marmaduke_Langdale" class="mw-redirect" title="Marmaduke Langdale">Marmaduke Langdale</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cumberland" title="Cumberland">Cumberland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Thanks to Lambert\'s successes, the Scottish commander, the <a href="/wiki/James_Hamilton,_3rd_Marquess_and_1st_Duke_of_Hamilton" class="mw-redirect" title="James Hamilton, 3rd Marquess and 1st Duke of Hamilton">Duke of Hamilton</a>, had perforce to take the western route through <a href="/wiki/Carlisle,_Cumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Carlisle, Cumbria">Carlisle</a> in his pro-Royalist Scottish invasion of England.<sup id="cite_ref-Newman-89_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newman-89-110">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The Parliamentarians under Cromwell engaged the Scots at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Preston_(1648)" title="Battle of Preston (1648)">Battle of Preston</a> (17\xe2\x80\x9319 August). The battle took place largely at <a href="/wiki/Walton-le-Dale" title="Walton-le-Dale">Walton-le-Dale</a> near <a href="/wiki/Preston,_Lancashire" title="Preston, Lancashire">Preston</a> in Lancashire, and resulted in a victory by the troops of Cromwell over the Royalists and Scots commanded by Hamilton.<sup id="cite_ref-Newman-89_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newman-89-110">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> This Parliamentarian victory marked the end of the Second English Civil War.\n</p><p>Nearly all the Royalists who had fought in the First Civil War had given their parole not to bear arms against the Parliament, and many of these, like <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Astley,_1st_Baron_Astley_of_Reading" title="Jacob Astley, 1st Baron Astley of Reading">Lord Astley</a>, refused to break their word by taking any part in the second war. So the victors in the Second Civil War showed little mercy to those who had brought war into the land again. On the evening of the surrender of Colchester, Parliamentarians had Sir <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lucas" title="Charles Lucas">Charles Lucas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sir_George_Lisle" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir George Lisle">Sir George Lisle</a> shot.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Parliamentary authorities sentenced the leaders of the Welsh rebels, Major-General <a href="/wiki/Rowland_Laugharne" title="Rowland Laugharne">Rowland Laugharne</a>, Colonel <a href="/wiki/John_Poyer" title="John Poyer">John Poyer</a> and Colonel <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rice_Powel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Rice Powel (page does not exist)">Rice Powel</a> to death, but executed Poyer alone (25 April 1649), having selected him by lot.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Of five prominent Royalist peers who had fallen into the hands of Parliament, three, the Duke of Hamilton, the <a href="/wiki/Henry_Rich,_1st_Earl_of_Holland" title="Henry Rich, 1st Earl of Holland">Earl of Holland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Capell,_1st_Baron_Capell_of_Hadham" title="Arthur Capell, 1st Baron Capell of Hadham">Lord Capel</a>, one of the Colchester prisoners and a man of high character, were beheaded at Westminster on 9 March.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trial_of_Charles_I_for_treason">Trial of Charles I for treason</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Trial of Charles I for treason">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Charles\' secret pacts and encouragement of his supporters to break their parole caused Parliament to debate whether to return the King to power at all. Those who still supported Charles\' place on the throne, such as the army leader and moderate Fairfax, tried once more to negotiate with him.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Furious that Parliament continued to countenance Charles as a ruler, the Army marched on Parliament and conducted "<a href="/wiki/Pride%27s_Purge" title="Pride&#39;s Purge">Pride\'s Purge</a>" (named after the commanding officer of the operation, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pride" title="Thomas Pride">Thomas Pride</a>) in December 1648.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Troops arrested 45 members of Parliament and kept 146 out of the chamber. They allowed only 75 Members in, and then only at the Army\'s bidding. This <a href="/wiki/Rump_Parliament" title="Rump Parliament">Rump Parliament</a> received orders to set up, in the name of the people of England, a <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice_for_the_trial_of_Charles_I" class="mw-redirect" title="High Court of Justice for the trial of Charles I">High Court of Justice for the trial of Charles I</a> for treason. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAylmer198025_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAylmer198025-116">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Fairfax, a constitutional monarchist and moderate, refused to participate whatsoever in the trial and resigned as head of the army, allowing Oliver Cromwell to ascend in power.\n</p><p>At the end of the trial the <a href="/wiki/List_of_regicides_of_Charles_I" title="List of regicides of Charles I">59 Commissioners</a> (judges) found Charles I guilty of <a href="/wiki/High_treason" title="High treason">high treason</a>, as a "tyrant, traitor, murderer and public enemy".<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Decapitation" title="Decapitation">beheading</a> took place on a scaffold in front of the <a href="/wiki/Banqueting_House" class="mw-redirect" title="Banqueting House">Banqueting House</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Whitehall" title="Palace of Whitehall">Palace of Whitehall</a> on 30 January 1649.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoyle2011&quot;Overview:_Civil_War_and_Revolution,_1603\xe2\x80\x931714&quot;_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoyle2011&quot;Overview:_Civil_War_and_Revolution,_1603\xe2\x80\x931714&quot;-119">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">Restoration</a> in 1660, of the surviving <a href="/wiki/List_of_regicides_of_Charles_I" title="List of regicides of Charles I">regicides</a> not living in exile, nine were executed and most of the rest sentenced to life imprisonment.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the execution, Charles, the eldest son was in <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">Jersey</a> where, on 17 February 1649 in the Royal Square in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helier" title="Saint Helier">St. Helier</a>, he was publicly proclaimed <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">King Charles II</a> (following the first public proclamation in Edinburgh on 5 February 1649).\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Third_English_Civil_War_.281649.E2.80.931651.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_English_Civil_War_(1649\xe2\x80\x931651)">Third English Civil War (1649\xe2\x80\x931651)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Third English Civil War (1649\xe2\x80\x931651)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Third_English_Civil_War" title="Third English Civil War">Third English Civil War</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cromwellian_conquest_of_Ireland" title="Cromwellian conquest of Ireland">Cromwellian conquest of Ireland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Massacre_at_Drogheda.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Massacre_at_Drogheda.jpeg/170px-Massacre_at_Drogheda.jpeg" width="170" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Massacre_at_Drogheda.jpeg/255px-Massacre_at_Drogheda.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Massacre_at_Drogheda.jpeg/340px-Massacre_at_Drogheda.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="487" data-file-height="660" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Massacre_at_Drogheda.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 19th century representation of the Massacre at <a href="/wiki/Drogheda" title="Drogheda">Drogheda</a>, 1649</div></div></div>\n<p>Ireland had known continuous war since the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1641" title="Irish Rebellion of 1641">rebellion of 1641</a>, with most of the island controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_Ireland" title="Confederate Ireland">Irish Confederates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Increasingly threatened by the armies of the English Parliament after Charles I\'s arrest in 1648, the Confederates signed a treaty of alliance with the English Royalists.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The joint Royalist and Confederate forces under <a href="/wiki/James_Butler,_1st_Duke_of_Ormonde" class="mw-redirect" title="James Butler, 1st Duke of Ormonde">the Duke of Ormonde</a> attempted to eliminate the Parliamentary army holding <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> by <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Dublin_(1649)" title="Siege of Dublin (1649)">laying siege to the city</a>, but their opponents routed them at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Rathmines" title="Battle of Rathmines">Battle of Rathmines</a> (2 August 1649).<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> As the former Member of Parliament <a href="/wiki/Admiral_Robert_Blake" class="mw-redirect" title="Admiral Robert Blake">Admiral Robert Blake</a> blockaded Prince Rupert\'s fleet in <a href="/wiki/Kinsale" title="Kinsale">Kinsale</a>, Oliver Cromwell could land at <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> on 15 August 1649 with an army to quell the Royalist alliance in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-Leniham-128_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leniham-128-124">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cromwell\'s suppression of the Royalists in Ireland during 1649 still has a strong resonance for many Irish people. After the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Drogheda" title="Siege of Drogheda">siege of Drogheda</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Leniham-128_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leniham-128-124">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> the massacre of nearly 3,500 people\xe2\x80\x94comprising around 2,700 Royalist soldiers and 700 others, including civilians, prisoners, and Catholic priests (Cromwell claimed all the men were carrying arms)\xe2\x80\x94became one of the historical memories that has driven Irish-English and Catholic-Protestant strife during the last three centuries. The <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliamentarian</a> conquest of Ireland ground on for another four years until 1653, when the last <a href="/wiki/Confederate_Ireland" title="Confederate Ireland">Irish Confederate</a> and Royalist troops surrendered.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The victors confiscated almost all Irish Catholic-owned land in the wake of the conquest and distributed it to the Parliament\'s creditors, to the Parliamentary soldiers who served in Ireland, and to English people who had settled there before the war.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland">Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Scotland_in_the_Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Scotland in the Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Scotland in the Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a></div>\n<p>The execution of <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a> altered the dynamics of <a href="/wiki/Scotland_in_the_Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Scotland in the Wars of the Three Kingdoms">the Civil War in Scotland</a>, which had raged between Royalists and <a href="/wiki/Covenanter" title="Covenanter">Covenanters</a> since 1644. By 1649, the struggle had left the Royalists there in disarray and their erstwhile leader, the <a href="/wiki/James_Graham,_1st_Marquess_of_Montrose" title="James Graham, 1st Marquess of Montrose">Marquess of Montrose</a>, had gone into exile. At first, <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a> encouraged Montrose to raise a Highland army to fight on the Royalist side.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter-145_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter-145-127">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> However, when the Scottish Covenanters (who did not agree with the execution of Charles I and who feared for the future of <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterianism</a> under the new <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth</a>) offered him the crown of Scotland, Charles abandoned Montrose to his enemies. However, Montrose, who had raised a <a href="/wiki/Mercenary" title="Mercenary">mercenary</a> force in Norway,<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter-145_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter-145-127">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> had already landed and could not abandon the fight. He did not succeed in raising many Highland clans and the Covenanters defeated his army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carbisdale_1650" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Carbisdale 1650">Battle of Carbisdale</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ross-shire" title="Ross-shire">Ross-shire</a> on 27 April 1650. The victors captured Montrose shortly afterwards and took him to <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>. On 20 May the Scottish Parliament sentenced him to death and had him hanged the next day.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter-146_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter-146-128">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg/220px-Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg/330px-Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg/440px-Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1235" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Cromwell at Dunbar", by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Carrick_Gow" title="Andrew Carrick Gow">Andrew Carrick Gow</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Charles II landed in Scotland at <a href="/wiki/Garmouth" class="mw-redirect" title="Garmouth">Garmouth</a> in <a href="/wiki/Morayshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Morayshire">Morayshire</a> on 23 June 1650<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> and signed the 1638 <a href="/wiki/National_Covenant" class="mw-redirect" title="National Covenant">National Covenant</a> and the 1643 <a href="/wiki/Solemn_League_and_Covenant" title="Solemn League and Covenant">Solemn League and Covenant</a> shortly after coming ashore.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> With his original Scottish Royalist followers and his new Covenanter allies, King Charles II became the greatest threat facing the new English republic. In response to the threat, Cromwell left some of his lieutenants in Ireland to continue the suppression of the Irish Royalists and returned to England.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter-146_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter-146-128">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He arrived in Scotland on 22 July 1650<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> and proceeded to lay siege to Edinburgh. By the end of August, disease and a shortage of supplies had reduced his army, and he had to order a retreat towards his base at <a href="/wiki/Dunbar" title="Dunbar">Dunbar</a>. A Scottish army, assembled under the command of <a href="/wiki/David_Leslie_(Scottish_general)" class="mw-redirect" title="David Leslie (Scottish general)">David Leslie</a>, tried to block the retreat, but Cromwell defeated them at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dunbar_(1650)" title="Battle of Dunbar (1650)">Battle of Dunbar</a> on 3 September. Cromwell\'s army then took Edinburgh, and by the end of the year his army had occupied much of southern Scotland.\n</p><p>In July 1651, Cromwell\'s forces crossed the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Firth of Forth</a> into <a href="/wiki/Fife" title="Fife">Fife</a> and defeated the Scots at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Inverkeithing" title="Battle of Inverkeithing">Battle of Inverkeithing</a> (20 July 1651).<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The New Model Army advanced towards <a href="/wiki/Perth,_Scotland" title="Perth, Scotland">Perth</a>, which allowed Charles, at the head of the Scottish army, to move south into England. Cromwell followed Charles into England, leaving <a href="/wiki/George_Monck" class="mw-redirect" title="George Monck">George Monck</a> to finish the campaign in Scotland. Monck took <a href="/wiki/Stirling" title="Stirling">Stirling</a> on 14 August and <a href="/wiki/Dundee" title="Dundee">Dundee</a> on 1 September.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> The next year, 1652, saw the mopping up of the remnants of Royalist resistance, and under the terms of the "<a href="/wiki/Tender_of_Union" title="Tender of Union">Tender of Union</a>", the Scots received 30 seats in a united Parliament in London, with General Monck appointed as the military governor of Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="England">England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Although Cromwell\'s New Model Army had defeated a Scottish army at Dunbar, Cromwell could not prevent Charles II from marching from Scotland deep into England at the head of another Royalist army.<sup id="cite_ref-Weiser-01_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weiser-01-135">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The Royalists marched to the west of England because English Royalist sympathies were strongest in that area, but although some English Royalists joined the army, they came in far fewer numbers than Charles and his Scottish supporters had hoped. Cromwell finally engaged and defeated the new king at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Worcester" title="Battle of Worcester">Worcester</a> on 3 September 1651.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter-145_127-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter-145-127">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAtkin2008&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2016&#93;&#93;&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing_sources&#124;&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2016)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAtkin2008[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2016]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2016)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-136">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immediate_aftermath">Immediate aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Immediate aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>After the Royalist defeat at Worcester, <a href="/wiki/Escape_of_Charles_II" title="Escape of Charles II">Charles II escaped</a>, via safe houses and a famous <a href="/wiki/Royal_Oak_(tree)" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Oak (tree)">oak tree</a>, to France,<sup id="cite_ref-Weiser-01_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weiser-01-135">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> and Parliament was left in <i>de facto</i> control of England. Resistance continued for a time <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands_in_the_Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Channel Islands in the Wars of the Three Kingdoms">in the Channel Islands</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Ireland and Scotland, but with the pacification of England the resistance elsewhere did not threaten the military supremacy of the New Model Army and its parliamentary paymasters.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_control">Political control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Political control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>During the Wars, the Parliamentarians established a number of successive committees to oversee the war-effort. The first of these, the <a href="/wiki/English_Committee_of_Safety" title="English Committee of Safety">Committee of Safety</a>, set up in July 1642, comprised 15 members of parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">Anglo</a>-Scottish alliance against the Royalists, the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Both_Kingdoms" title="Committee of Both Kingdoms">Committee of Both Kingdoms</a> replaced the Committee of Safety between 1644 and 1648.<sup id="cite_ref-Kennedy-96_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy-96-139">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Parliament dissolved the Committee of Both Kingdoms when the alliance ended, but its English members continued to meet and became known as the <a href="/wiki/Derby_House_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="Derby House Committee">Derby House Committee</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kennedy-96_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy-96-139">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> A second Committee of Safety then replaced that committee.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Episcopacy">Episcopacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Episcopacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(May 2012)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Laud.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/William_Laud.jpg/220px-William_Laud.jpg" width="220" height="291" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/William_Laud.jpg/330px-William_Laud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/William_Laud.jpg/440px-William_Laud.jpg 2x" data-file-width="604" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Laud.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Laud" title="William Laud">William Laud</a>, Charles I\'s Archbishop of Canterbury.</div></div></div>\n<p>During the period of the English Civil War, the role of bishops as wielders of political power and as upholders of the <a href="/wiki/Established_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Established church">established church</a> became a matter of heated political controversy. <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> formulated a doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterianism</a>, which held that in the New Testament the offices of <i>presbyter</i> and <i>episkopos</i> were identical; he rejected the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_succession" title="Apostolic succession">apostolic succession</a>. Calvin\'s follower <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a> brought Presbyterianism to <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> when the Scottish church was reformed in 1560. In practice, Presbyterianism meant that committees of lay elders had a substantial voice in church government, as opposed to merely being subjects to a ruling <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">hierarchy</a>.\n</p><p>This vision of at least partial <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiology" title="Ecclesiology">ecclesiology</a> paralleled the struggles between <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_monarchs" title="List of English monarchs">the King</a>. A body within the <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> movement in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> sought to abolish the office of bishop and remake the Church of England along Presbyterian lines. The <a href="/wiki/Martin_Marprelate" title="Martin Marprelate">Martin Marprelate</a> tracts (1588\xe2\x80\x931589), applying the <a href="/wiki/Pejorative" title="Pejorative">pejorative</a> name of <i><a href="/wiki/Prelate" title="Prelate">prelacy</a></i> to the church hierarchy, attacked the office of bishop with satire that deeply offended <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a> and her <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Whitgift" title="John Whitgift">John Whitgift</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Vestments_controversy" title="Vestments controversy">vestments controversy</a> also related to this movement, seeking further reductions in church ceremony, and labelling the use of elaborate vestments as "unedifying" and even <a href="/wiki/Idolatry_and_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Idolatry and Christianity">idolatrous</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">King James I</a>, reacting against the perceived contumacy of his Presbyterian Scottish subjects, adopted "No Bishop, no King" as a slogan; he tied the hierarchical authority of the bishop to the absolute authority he sought as king, and viewed attacks on the authority of the bishops as attacks on his own authority. Matters came to a head when King <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a> appointed <a href="/wiki/William_Laud" title="William Laud">William Laud</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>; Laud aggressively attacked the Presbyterian movement and sought to impose the full <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Anglican liturgy</a>. The controversy eventually led to Laud\'s <a href="/wiki/Impeachment" title="Impeachment">impeachment</a> for <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treason</a> by a <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_attainder" title="Bill of attainder">bill of attainder</a> in 1645, and subsequent execution. Charles also attempted to impose episcopacy on Scotland; the Scots\' violent rejection of bishops and liturgical worship sparked the <a href="/wiki/Bishops%27_Wars" title="Bishops&#39; Wars">Bishops\' Wars</a> in 1639\xe2\x80\x931640.\n</p><p>During the height of Puritan power in <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">the Commonwealth</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Protectorate" title="The Protectorate">the Protectorate</a>, episcopacy was formally abolished in the Church of England on 9 October 1646.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing1968523\xe2\x80\x9337_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing1968523\xe2\x80\x9337-140">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> The Church of England remained Presbyterian until the <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">Restoration</a> of the monarchy with <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a> in 1660.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="English_overseas_possessions">English overseas possessions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: English overseas possessions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/English_overseas_possessions_in_the_Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="English overseas possessions in the Wars of the Three Kingdoms">English overseas possessions in the Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a></div>\n<p>During the period of the English Civil War, the English overseas possessions were highly involved. In the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands_in_the_Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Channel Islands in the Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Channel Islands</a>, the island of Jersey and <a href="/wiki/Castle_Cornet" title="Castle Cornet">Castle Cornet</a> in Guernsey supported the King until in December 1651 they surrendered with honour. Although the newer, Puritan settlements in North America, most notably <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>, were dominated by Parliamentarians, the older colonies sided with the Crown. Friction between royalists and Puritans in Maryland came to a head in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Severn" title="Battle of the Severn">Battle of the Severn</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Company_of_London" class="mw-redirect" title="Virginia Company of London">Virginia Company\'s</a> settlements, <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Antigua" title="Antigua">Antigua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a> were conspicuous in their loyalty to the Crown. Bermuda\'s Independent Puritans were expelled, settling the <a href="/wiki/Bahamas" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahamas">Bahamas</a> under <a href="/wiki/William_Sayle" title="William Sayle">William Sayle</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Eleutheran_Adventurers" title="Eleutheran Adventurers">Eleutheran Adventurers</a>. Parliament passed <a href="/wiki/An_Act_for_prohibiting_Trade_with_the_Barbadoes,_Virginia,_Bermuda_and_Antego" title="An Act for prohibiting Trade with the Barbadoes, Virginia, Bermuda and Antego">An Act for prohibiting Trade with the Barbadoes, Virginia, Bermuda and Antego</a> in October, 1650, which stated that\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>due punishment [be] inflicted upon the said Delinquents, do Declare all and every the said persons in Barbada\'s, Antego, Bermuda\'s and Virginia, that have contrived, abetted, aided or assisted those horrid Rebellions, or have since willingly joyned with them, to be notorious Robbers and Traitors, and such as by the Law of Nations are not to be permitted any maner of Commerce or Traffique with any people whatsoever; and do forbid to all maner of persons, Foreiners, and others, all maner of Commerce, Traffique and Correspondency whatsoever, to be used or held with the said Rebels in the Barbada\'s, Bermuda\'s, Virginia and Antego, or either of them.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The Act also authorised Parliamentary <a href="/wiki/Privateer" title="Privateer">privateers</a> to act against English vessels trading with the rebellious colonies:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>All Ships that Trade with the Rebels may be surprized. Goods and tackle of such ships not to be embezeled, till judgement in the Admiralty.; Two or three of the Officers of every ship to be examined upon oath.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The Parliament began assembling a fleet to invade the Royalist colonies, but many of the English islands in the Caribbean were captured by the Dutch and French in 1651 during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Dutch_War" title="Second Anglo-Dutch War">Second Anglo-Dutch War</a>. Far to the North, Bermuda\'s regiment of Militia and its coastal batteries prepared to resist an invasion that never came. The colony made a separate peace that respected its internal status quo. The <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Bermuda" title="Parliament of Bermuda">Parliament of Bermuda</a> avoided the Parliament of England\'s fate during <a href="/wiki/The_Protectorate" title="The Protectorate">The Protectorate</a>, becoming one of the oldest continuous legislatures in the world.\n</p><p>Virginia\'s population swelled with Cavaliers during and after the English Civil War. Even so, Virginia Puritan <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bennett_(Governor)" title="Richard Bennett (Governor)">Richard Bennett</a> was made Governor answering to Cromwell in 1652, followed by two more nominal "Commonwealth Governors". The loyalty of Virginia\'s <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Cavaliers_(historical)" title="Virginia Cavaliers (historical)">Cavaliers</a> to the Crown was rewarded after the 1660 <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">Restoration of the Monarchy</a> when King Charles II dubbed it the <i>Old Dominion</i>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Casualties">Casualties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Casualties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Figures for casualties during this period are unreliable, but some attempt has been made to provide rough estimates.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Carlton-211-214_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlton-211-214-143">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\nIn England, a conservative estimate is that roughly 100,000 people died from war-related disease during the three civil wars. Historical records count 84,830 dead from the wars themselves. Counting in accidents and the two Bishops\' wars, an estimate of 190,000 dead is achieved,<sup id="cite_ref-Carlton-211_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlton-211-144">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> out of a total population of about five million.<sup id="cite_ref-James-187_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James-187-145">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Figures for Scotland are less reliable and should be treated with greater caution. Casualties include the deaths of prisoners-of-war in conditions that accelerated their deaths, with estimates of 10,000 prisoners not surviving or not returning home (8,000 captured during and immediately after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Worcester" title="Battle of Worcester">Battle of Worcester</a> were deported to <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a> and the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a> to work for landowners as <a href="/wiki/Indentured_labour" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentured labour">indentured labourers</a><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>). There are no figures to calculate how many died from war-related diseases, but if the same ratio of disease to battle deaths from English figures is applied to the Scottish figures, a not unreasonable estimate of 60,000 people is achieved,<sup id="cite_ref-Carlton-212_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlton-212-147">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> from a population of about one million.<sup id="cite_ref-James-187_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James-187-145">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Figures for Ireland are described as "miracles of conjecture". Certainly the devastation inflicted on Ireland was massive, with the best estimate provided by Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Petty" title="William Petty">William Petty</a>, the father of English demography. Petty estimates that 112,000 Protestants and 504,000 Catholics were killed through <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">plague</a>, war and <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>, giving an estimated total of 616,000 dead,<sup id="cite_ref-Carlton-213_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlton-213-148">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> from a pre-war population of about one and a half million.<sup id="cite_ref-James-187_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James-187-145">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Although Petty\'s figures are the best available, they are still acknowledged as being tentative; they do not include the estimate of 40,000 driven into exile, some of whom served as soldiers in European continental armies, while others were sold as indentured servants to New England and the West Indies. Many of those sold to landowners in New England eventually prospered, but many of those sold to landowners in the West Indies were worked to death.\n</p><p>These estimates indicate that England suffered a 3.7% loss of population, Scotland a loss of 6%, while Ireland suffered a loss of 41% of its population. Putting these numbers into the context of other catastrophes helps to understand the devastation to Ireland in particular. The <a href="/wiki/Great_Hunger" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Hunger">Great Hunger</a> of 1845\xe2\x80\x931852 resulted in a loss of 16% of the population, while during the Second World War the population of the Soviet Union fell by 16%.<sup id="cite_ref-Carlton-214_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlton-214-149">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_gains">Popular gains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Popular gains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Ordinary people took advantage of the dislocation of civil society during the 1640s to derive advantages for themselves. The contemporary guild democracy movement won its greatest successes among London\'s transport workers, notably the Thames <a href="/wiki/Watermen" class="mw-redirect" title="Watermen">watermen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Rural communities seized timber and other resources on the sequestrated estates of royalists and Catholics, and on the estates of the royal family and the church hierarchy. Some communities improved their conditions of tenure on such estates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Riordan1993184\xe2\x80\x93200_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Riordan1993184\xe2\x80\x93200-151">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> The old <i>status quo</i> began a retrenchment after the end of the First Civil War in 1646, and more especially after the restoration of monarchy in 1660. But some gains were long-term. The democratic element introduced in the watermen\'s company in 1642, for example, survived, with vicissitudes, until 1827.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The wars left England, Scotland, and Ireland among the few countries in Europe without a monarch. In the wake of victory, many of the ideals (and many of the idealists) became sidelined. The republican government of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth of England</a> ruled England (and later all of Scotland and Ireland) from 1649 to 1653 and from 1659 to 1660. Between the two periods, and due to in-fighting among various factions in Parliament, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> ruled over <a href="/wiki/The_Protectorate" title="The Protectorate">the Protectorate</a> as <a href="/wiki/Lord_Protector" title="Lord Protector">Lord Protector</a> (effectively a military dictator) until his death in 1658.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Upon his death, Oliver Cromwell\'s son <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cromwell" title="Richard Cromwell">Richard</a> became Lord Protector, but the Army had little confidence in him.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> After seven months the Army removed Richard, and in May 1659 it re-installed the Rump.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> However, since the Rump Parliament acted as though nothing had changed since 1653 and as though it could treat the Army as it liked, military force shortly afterwards dissolved this, as well.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> After the second dissolution of the Rump, in October 1659, the prospect of a total descent into anarchy loomed as the Army\'s pretence of unity finally dissolved into factions.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Musket_volley_by_Sealed_Knot.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Musket_volley_by_Sealed_Knot.JPG/220px-Musket_volley_by_Sealed_Knot.JPG" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Musket_volley_by_Sealed_Knot.JPG/330px-Musket_volley_by_Sealed_Knot.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Musket_volley_by_Sealed_Knot.JPG/440px-Musket_volley_by_Sealed_Knot.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2894" data-file-height="2029" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Musket_volley_by_Sealed_Knot.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A historical civil war re-enactment</div></div></div>\n<p>Into this atmosphere General <a href="/wiki/George_Monck,_1st_Duke_of_Albemarle" title="George Monck, 1st Duke of Albemarle">George Monck</a>, Governor of Scotland under the Cromwells, marched south with his army from Scotland. On 4 April 1660, in the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Breda" title="Declaration of Breda">Declaration of Breda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a> made known the conditions of his acceptance of the Crown of England.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Monck organised the <a href="/wiki/Convention_Parliament_(1660)" title="Convention Parliament (1660)">Convention Parliament</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> which met for the first time on 25 April 1660. On 8 May 1660, it declared that King Charles II had reigned as the lawful monarch since the execution of Charles I in January 1649. Charles returned from exile on 23 May 1660. On 29 May 1660, the populace in London acclaimed him as king.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> His coronation took place at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> on 23 April 1661. These events became known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Restoration_(England)" title="Restoration (England)">Restoration</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although the monarchy was restored, it was still only with the consent of Parliament; therefore, the civil wars effectively set England and Scotland on course to adopt a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_monarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary monarchy">parliamentary monarchy</a> form of government.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> This system would result in the outcome that the future <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>, formed in 1707 under the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1707" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1707">Acts of Union</a>, would manage to forestall the kind of often-bloody revolution, typical of European republican movements that followed the <a href="/wiki/Jacobin_(politics)" title="Jacobin (politics)">Jacobin</a> revolution in 18th century France and the later success of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>, which generally resulted in the total abolition of monarchy. It was no coincidence that the United Kingdom was spared the wave of revolutions that occurred in Europe in the 1840s. Specifically, future monarchs became wary of pushing Parliament too hard, and Parliament effectively chose the line of royal succession in 1688 with the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> and in the 1701 <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Settlement_1701" title="Act of Settlement 1701">Act of Settlement</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">Restoration</a>, Parliament\'s factions became political parties (later becoming the <a href="/wiki/Tory" title="Tory">Tories</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whig_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Whig (UK)">Whigs</a>) with competing views and varying abilities to influence the decisions of their monarchs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="For the whole paragraph (May 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography_and_explanations">Historiography and explanations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Historiography and explanations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks old nibba" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;border-collapse:collapse;"><tbody><tr><td style="background-color:#B2BEB5;;padding-top:0.4em;line-height:1.2em">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Puritans" title="Category:Puritans">a series</a> on</td></tr><tr><th style="background-color:#B2BEB5;;padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;padding-top:0;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.2em 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="5472" /></a><div style="padding-top:0.2em;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/The_Puritan_(Springfield)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Puritan (Springfield)"><i>The Puritan</i></a> (1887), a statue in <a href="/wiki/Springfield,_Massachusetts" title="Springfield, Massachusetts">Springfield, Massachusetts</a> by <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Saint-Gaudens" title="Augustus Saint-Gaudens">Augustus Saint-Gaudens</a></div></td></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" Frankfurt">Troubles at Frankfurt</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background-color:#B2BEB5;;background:#B2BEB5;padding:0.2em &#39;&#39;&#39;Bold text&#39;&#39;&#39;;">Works</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-left:0.2em; padding-right:0.2em;">\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Godly_Man%27s_Picture" title="The Godly Man&#39;s Picture">The Martyrs</a></i></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background-color:#B2BEB5;;background:#B2BEB5;padding:0.2em &#39;&#39;&#39;Bold text&#39;&#39;&#39;;">Continuing movements</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-left:0.2em; padding-right:0.2em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Congregational_churches" class="mw-redirect" title="Congregational churches">Congregational churches</a> (<small><a href="/wiki/Congregationalism_in_the_United_States" title="Congregationalism in the United States">U.S.</a></small>)</li>\n<li>other <a href="/wiki/Reformed_churches" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed churches">Reformed churches</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Fat_nibba&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Template:Fat nibba (page does not exist)"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:Fat_nibba&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Template talk:Fat nibba (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Fat_nibba&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In the early decades of the 20th century the <a href="/wiki/Whig_history" title="Whig history">Whig school</a> was the dominant theoretical view. They explained the Civil War as resulting from a centuries-long struggle between Parliament (especially the House of Commons) and the Monarchy, with Parliament defending the traditional rights of Englishmen, while the Stuart monarchy continually attempted to expand its right to arbitrarily dictate law. The most important Whig historian, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Rawson_Gardiner" title="Samuel Rawson Gardiner">S.R. Gardiner</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (April 2017)">full citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> popularised the idea that the English Civil War was a "Puritan Revolution": challenging the repressive Stuart Church, and preparing the way for <a href="/wiki/Religious_toleration" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious toleration">religious toleration</a> in the Restoration. Thus, Puritanism was the natural ally of a people preserving their traditional rights against arbitrary monarchical power.\n</p><p>The Whig view was challenged and largely superseded by the <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxist</a> school, which became popular in the 1940s, and which interpreted the English Civil War as a <a href="/wiki/Bourgeois" class="mw-redirect" title="Bourgeois">bourgeois</a> revolution. According to Marxist historian <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hill_(historian)" title="Christopher Hill (historian)">Christopher Hill</a>:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Civil War was a class war, in which the despotism of Charles I was defended by the reactionary forces of the established Church and conservative landlords, Parliament beat the King because it could appeal to the enthusiastic support of the trading and industrial classes in town and countryside, to the yeomen and progressive gentry, and to wider masses of the population whenever they were able by free discussion to understand what the struggle was really about.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In the 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Historical_revisionism" title="Historical revisionism">revisionist historians</a> challenged both the Whig and the Marxist theories,<sup id="cite_ref-Burgess_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burgess-164">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> notably in the 1973 anthology <i>The Origins of the English Civil War</i> (<a href="/wiki/Conrad_Russell" class="mw-redirect" title="Conrad Russell">Conrad Russell</a> ed.).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1973&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_April_2017&#93;&#93;&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing_sources&#124;&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(April_2017)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1973[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_April_2017]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(April_2017)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-165">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> These historians produced work focused on the minutiae of the years immediately preceding the civil war, thereby returning to the contingency-based historiography of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hyde,_1st_Earl_of_Clarendon" title="Edward Hyde, 1st Earl of Clarendon">Clarendon\'s</a> famous contemporary history <i>History of the Rebellion and Civil Wars in England</i>.<a href="#CITEREFGaunt2000">Gaunt 2000</a>, p.&#160;60 This, it was claimed, demonstrated that factional war-allegiance patterns did not fit either Whig or Marxist history.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Parliament was not inherently progressive, with the events of 1640 a precursor for the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> nor did Puritans necessarily ally themselves with Parliamentarians.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Many members of the bourgeoisie fought for the King, while many landed aristocrats supported Parliament. Thus, revisionist historians claim to have discredited some Whig and Marxist interpretations of the English Civil War.<sup id="cite_ref-Burgess_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burgess-164">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (June 2008)">not in citation given</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>From the 1990s, a number of historians discarded and replaced the historical title "English Civil War" with the titles the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a> and the "British Civil Wars", positing that the civil war in England cannot be understood isolated from events in other parts of Great Britain and Ireland; King Charles I remains crucial, not just as King of England, but also because of his relationship with the peoples of his other realms. For example, the wars began when King Charles I tried imposing an Anglican Prayer Book upon Scotland, and when this was met with resistance from the <a href="/wiki/Covenanter" title="Covenanter">Covenanters</a>, he needed an army to impose his will. However, this forced him to call an English Parliament to raise new taxes to pay for the army. The English Parliaments were not willing to grant Charles the revenue he needed to pay for the Scottish expeditionary army unless he addressed their grievances. By the early 1640s, Charles was left in a state of near permanent crisis management; often he was not willing to concede enough ground to any one faction to neutralise the threat, and in some circumstances to do so would only antagonise another faction. For example, Charles finally agreed upon terms with the Covenanters in August 1641, but although this might have weakened the position of the English Parliament, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1641" title="Irish Rebellion of 1641">Irish Rebellion of 1641</a> broke out in October 1641, largely negating the political advantage he had obtained by relieving himself of the cost of the Scottish invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOhlmeyer2002_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOhlmeyer2002-168">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> gives a much earlier historical account of the English Civil War in his essay <a href="/wiki/Behemoth_(Hobbes_book)" title="Behemoth (Hobbes book)">Behemoth</a>, written in 1668 and published in 1681. He reports that the causes of the war were the doctrines of politics and conflicts that arose from science that disputed those political doctrines.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHobbes1839220_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHobbes1839220-169">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Behemoth offered a uniquely historical and philosophical approach to naming the catalysts for the war. It also served as a political statement to explain why King Charles I was incapable of holding his place of power and maintaining peace in his kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKraynak199033_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKraynak199033-170">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Specifically, Hobbes analyses the following aspects of English thought during the war (listed in order of his discussions in Behemoth): the opinions of divinity and politics that spurred rebellion; rhetoric and doctrine used by the rebels against the king; and how opinions about "taxation, the conscription of soldiers, and military strategy" affected the outcomes of battles and shifts of sovereignty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKraynak199033_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKraynak199033-170">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hobbes offered a unique contribution to historical interpretation of the civil war through his Behemoth by connecting the civil war to the motivations of intellectuals who Hobbes reports caused it by trying to spread certain ideas throughout the nation, largely for the sake of displaying their own wisdom and learning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldsmith1966x\xe2\x80\x93xiii_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldsmith1966x\xe2\x80\x93xiii-171">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hobbes held the belief that clerical pretensions had contributed significantly to the trouble during the civil war\xe2\x80\x94"whether those of puritan fundamentalists, papal supremacists or divine right Episcopalians".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESommerville2012_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESommerville2012-172">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Hobbes wanted to revoke all of independent power of the clergy and to change the civil system such that they were controlled by the state.\n</p><p>Some scholars suggest that Behemoth has not received its due respect as an academic work, being comparatively overlooked and underrated in the shadow of <a href="/wiki/Leviathan_(Hobbes_book)" title="Leviathan (Hobbes book)">Leviathan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKraynak1990_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKraynak1990-173">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2016)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacgillivray1970179_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacgillivray1970179-174">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> One factor that may have contributed to its lack of reception as a historical work is that it takes the form of a dialogue. While philosophical dialogues are common, historical ones are not. Other factors that hindered its success include King Charles II\'s refusing its publication and Hobbes\' chiefly interpretive approach to the historical narrative.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacgillivray1970179_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacgillivray1970179-174">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Much can be gleaned about Hobbes as a person from looking at the difficulties he faced while seeking an audience for Behemoth. The essay illuminates a flaw shared by most of Hobbes\'s political philosophy as well, which is his lack of ability or willingness to empathize with perspectives that largely differed from his own. As his perspective was so much at odds with other views, Hobbes struggled to understand the thinking of most of his potential audience and people in general.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacgillivray1970179_174-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacgillivray1970179-174">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> For instance, he accredits the Presbyterians and Parliamentarians with "improbably long-planned and wicked ambitions".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacgillivray1970179_174-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacgillivray1970179-174">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> What\'s more, "he hardly understands the orthodox Royalists (he was himself a highly unorthodox Royalist) any better, and he makes only limited concessions of sincerity to the religious feelings of the various parties".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacgillivray1970179_174-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacgillivray1970179-174">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-enactments">Re-enactments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Re-enactments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Civil_war_reeanactment.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Civil_war_reeanactment.JPG/220px-Civil_war_reeanactment.JPG" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Civil_war_reeanactment.JPG/330px-Civil_war_reeanactment.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Civil_war_reeanactment.JPG/440px-Civil_war_reeanactment.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="460" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Civil_war_reeanactment.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A historical civil war re-enactment</div></div></div>\n<p>Two large historical societies exist, <a href="/wiki/The_Sealed_Knot_(reenactment)" title="The Sealed Knot (reenactment)">The Sealed Knot</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_English_Civil_War_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="The English Civil War Society">The English Civil War Society</a>, which regularly <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War_reenactment" class="mw-redirect" title="English Civil War reenactment">re-enact</a> events and battles of the Civil War in full period costume.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_English_Civil_War" title="Timeline of the English Civil War">Timeline of the English Civil War</a>, showing events leading up to, culminating in, and resulting from the English Civil Wars.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/First_English_Civil_War,_1642" title="First English Civil War, 1642">First English Civil War, 1642</a></li>\n<li><a title="Levellers">Levellers</a>, a movement for political reform.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Diggers" title="Diggers">Diggers</a></li></ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Civil_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While it is notoriously difficult to determine the number of casualties in any war, it has been estimated that the conflict in England and Wales claimed about 85,000 lives in combat, with a further 127,000 noncombat deaths (including some 40,000 civilians)" (<a href="#CITEREFEB_staff2016b">EB staff 2016b</a>)  </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although the early 17th century Stuart monarchs styled themselves King of Great Britain, France and Ireland, with the exception of the constitutional arrangements during the <a href="/wiki/Interregnum_(England)" title="Interregnum (England)">Interregnum</a> (see the <a href="/wiki/Tender_of_Union" title="Tender of Union">Tender of Union</a>), full union of the Scottish and English realms into a new realm of Great Britain did not occur until the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1707" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1707">Act of Union 1707</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a href="#CITEREFWalter1999">Walter 1999</a>, p.&#160;294, for some of the complexities of how the Protestation was interpreted by different political actors.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Cromwell had already secured Cambridge and the supplies of college silver (<a href="#CITEREFWedgwood1970">Wedgwood 1970</a>, p.&#160;106).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-153"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-153">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For a longer analysis of the relationship between Cromwell\'s position, the former monarchy and the military, see <a href="#CITEREFSherwood1997">Sherwood 1997</a>, pp.&#160;7\xe2\x80\x9311.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<div
